<div class="ltx_page_content">
<article class="ltx_document">
<h1 class="ltx_title ltx_title_document">A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells</h1>
<div class="ltx_authors">
<span class="ltx_creator ltx_role_author">
<span class="ltx_personname">Gabriel L. Nogueira
</span><span class="ltx_author_notes">
<span class="ltx_contact ltx_role_affiliation">Department of Physics and Meteorology, School of Sciences, Sao Paulo State University (UNESP), Bauru, Sao Paulo, Brazil 
</span>
<span class="ltx_contact ltx_role_email"><a href="mailto:leonardo.nogueira@unesp.br">leonardo.nogueira@unesp.br</a>
</span></span></span>
<span class="ltx_author_before">  </span><span class="ltx_creator ltx_role_author">
<span class="ltx_personname">Victor Lopez-Richard
</span><span class="ltx_author_notes">
<span class="ltx_contact ltx_role_affiliation">Department of Physics, Federal University of Sao Carlos (UFSCar), Sao Carlos, Sao Paulo, Brazil
</span></span></span>
<span class="ltx_author_before">  </span><span class="ltx_creator ltx_role_author">
<span class="ltx_personname">Luiz A. Meneghetti Jr
</span><span class="ltx_author_notes">
<span class="ltx_contact ltx_role_affiliation">Department of Physics, Federal University of Sao Carlos (UFSCar), Sao Carlos, Sao Paulo, Brazil

</span></span></span>
<span class="ltx_author_before">  </span><span class="ltx_creator ltx_role_author">

</span><span class="ltx_author_notes">

</span>
<span class="ltx_contact ltx_role_email"><a href="mailto:carlos.graeff@unesp.br">carlos.graeff@unesp.br</a>
</span></span></span>
</div>
<div class="ltx_abstract">
<h6 class="ltx_title ltx_title_abstract">Abstract</h6>
<p class="ltx_p" id="id8.8">Perovskite solar cells (PSCs) have emerged as a promising photovoltaic technology, already achieving efficiencies surpassing 25%. However, effects such as hysteresis are commonly observed due to the interplay of ionic and electronic transport occurring over different timescales. Despite the widespread use of impedance spectroscopy (IS), physical interpretation in PSCs is specially challenging due to memory effects. In this study, we focus on integrating experimental data with an analytical device transport model. The PSCs under investigation were fabricated using a Cs<sub class="ltx_sub" id="id8.8.1"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="id8.8.1.1">0.17</span></sub>FA<sub class="ltx_sub" id="id8.8.2"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="id8.8.2.1">0.83</span></sub>Pb(I<sub class="ltx_sub" id="id8.8.3"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="id8.8.3.1">0.83</span></sub>Br<sub class="ltx_sub" id="id8.8.4"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="id8.8.4.1">0.17</span></sub>)<sub class="ltx_sub" id="id8.8.5">3</sub> active layer between Nb<sub class="ltx_sub" id="id8.8.6">2</sub>O<sub class="ltx_sub" id="id8.8.7">5</sub>/TiO<sub class="ltx_sub" id="id8.8.8">2</sub> (compact/mesoporous) and Spiro-OMeTAD. Our fully analytical charge transport model incorporates independent charge transport channels, enabling the correlation of experimental observations in both dark and under illumination. We employed IS together with various voltammetry techniques to reveal the dynamics of the transport processes, including voltage pulses and both small and large amplitude sinusoidal excitations. Although small perturbations are commonly used in IS, our findings demonstrate that large sinusoidal excitation provides new valuable insights in the transition from capacitive to inductive-like responses. The proposed model based purely on charge trapping and generation effectively captures device behavior under both small and large voltages without inductive elements, validated through accurate simulations of hysteresis and other electrical phenomena.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_classification">
<h6 class="ltx_title ltx_title_classification">keywords: </h6>Perovskite Solar Cell, Hysteresis, Spectral Analysis, Large Perturbation, Analytical model
</div>
<section class="ltx_section" id="S1">
<h2 class="ltx_title ltx_title_section">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">1 </span>Introduction</h2>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p1.1">Metal halide perovskites have garnered significant attention for their potential in solar cell applications, driven by remarkable performance improvements in recent years. Perovskite solar cells (PSCs) have achieved power conversion efficiencies (PCEs) exceeding 25%, a milestone that, in combination with their ease of fabrication, has placed them at the forefront of emergent photovoltaic technologies <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p1.1.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib1" title="">1</a></sup></cite>. Despite this impressive performance, perovskite-based photovoltaic devices face challenges related to stability and metastability, which complicate, for example, the accurate assessment of solar-to-electrical energy conversion efficiency <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p1.1.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib2" title="">2</a></sup></cite>. A prominent issue is the hysteresis in the current-voltage curves, which manifests as discrepancies between the forward and reverse voltage sweeps <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p1.1.3.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib4" title="">4</a></sup></cite>. These effects have prompted extensive research into the fundamental properties of halide perovskites, intending to unlock their full potential for sustainable energy conversion.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S1.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p2.3">These materials exhibit dual ionic-electronic transport. Typically, electronic responses occur within hundreds of nanoseconds, while the redistribution of mobile ions unfolds over seconds to minutes. Anta et al. <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p2.3.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib3" title="">3</a></sup></cite> highlighted this interplay, showing how external stimuli influence ionic movement and how slow reorganization of ions can influence electronic transport. Tress et al., linked hysteresis in CH<sub class="ltx_sub" id="S1.p2.3.2">3</sub>NH<sub class="ltx_sub" id="S1.p2.3.3">3</sub>PbI<sub class="ltx_sub" id="S1.p2.3.4">3</sub> to ion diffusion and charge carrier collection efficiency <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p2.3.5.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib5" title="">5</a></sup></cite>. These dynamics are regarded as a key factor underlying hysteresis in current-voltage (J-V) measurements <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p2.3.6.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib6" title="">6</a></sup></cite>. The J-V characteristics of PSCs are sensitive to parameters such as voltage sweep rate, preconditioning protocols, and sweep direction. Techniques such as impedance spectroscopy (IS) have been instrumental in elucidating the interactions between ionic and electronic processes <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p2.3.7.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib7" title="">7</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib8" title="">8</a></sup></cite>. Additionally, transport models, including atomistic simulations and drift-diffusion models, have furthered our understanding of these complex phenomena <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p2.3.8.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib3" title="">3</a></sup></cite>.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S1.p3">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p3.1">Recent studies draw parallels between PSCs and memristors, suggesting that the resistive switching behavior commonly observed in memristors may naturally occur in PSCs due to ionic-controlled interface recombination <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p3.1.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib9" title="">9</a></sup></cite> and other transport mechanisms. This underscores the suitability of using memristor models in systems involving materials with mobile ions <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p3.1.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib6" title="">6</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib10" title="">10</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib11" title="">11</a></sup></cite>. A memristor is a non-linear electrical component whose resistance depends on the history of applied voltage and current, effectively “remembering” past electrical states <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p3.1.3.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib12" title="">12</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib13" title="">13</a></sup></cite>. In PSCs, the slow redistribution of ions under external stimuli leads to resistive switching effects, analogous to memristive devices <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p3.1.4.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib13" title="">13</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib14" title="">14</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib15" title="">15</a></sup></cite>. The architecture of PSC device has a strong effect on hysteresis. In planar PSCs, hysteresis is often attributed to inefficient charge transfer at the perovskite-transport layer interface, leading to charge trapping and recombination <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p3.1.5.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib16" title="">16</a></sup></cite>. Mesoporous PSCs, while exhibiting improved charge extraction compared to planar architectures, often show more pronounced hysteresis than typically observed in p-i-n devices <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S1.p3.1.6.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib17" title="">17</a></sup></cite>.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S1.p4">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S1.p4.1">In this study, we integrate multiple experiments with a transport model to investigate hysteresis in PSCs. Beyond standard electrical characterization, we employ both small AC perturbations and large amplitude voltage stimuli to probe the dynamics of transport processes. Our model provides a comprehensive framework for describing the anomalous effects observed in PSCs, moving beyond traditional equivalent circuit models.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_section" id="S2">
<h2 class="ltx_title ltx_title_section">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">2 </span>Results and discussion</h2>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S2.SS1">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.1 </span>Experimental results</h3>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS1.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS1.p1.3">The J-V curves were obtained by sweeping the applied voltage over a specific range. Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F1" title="Figure 1 ‣ 2.1 Experimental results ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>a and Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F1" title="Figure 1 ‣ 2.1 Experimental results ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>b show forward and reverse bias voltage scans from representative devices, in which hysteresis is clearly observed. consistent with prior reports <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p1.3.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib18" title="">18</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib19" title="">19</a></sup></cite>. The observed counterclockwise hysteresis is classified as normal <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p1.3.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib24" title="">24</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib25" title="">25</a></sup></cite> and becomes more pronounced at faster sweep rates. In forward sweep direction, the effect of the scan rate on PCE is especially critical, as provided in Figs. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F1" title="Figure 1 ‣ 2.1 Experimental results ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>c and <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F1" title="Figure 1 ‣ 2.1 Experimental results ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>d.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS1.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS1.p2.1">Impedance spectroscopy is commonly used in PSCs, often complemented by equivalent circuit models. These models represent the dominant components of the system as resistors, capacitors and inductors, along with their interconnection. Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F1" title="Figure 1 ‣ 2.1 Experimental results ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>e illustrates the IS measurements performed at 0.9 V, close to the open-circuit condition, both in the dark and under illumination at two different light intensities (14 and 72 mWcm<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p2.1.1"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS1.p2.1.1.1">-2</span></sup>). The dark measurement reveals a low frequency loop, consistent with previous reports of a pseudo-inductive response <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p2.1.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib26" title="">26</a></sup></cite>.</p>
</div>

<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS1.p3">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS1.p3.1">IS data can also be analyzed using Bode plots, where the focus is on frequency response. In Figs. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F1" title="Figure 1 ‣ 2.1 Experimental results ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>f and <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F1" title="Figure 1 ‣ 2.1 Experimental results ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>g, we show the spectral analysis of conductance and susceptance derived from the IS data. The conductance increases proportionally with illumination intensity, while in the dark it increases as the frequency decreases. To better observe the high-frequency behavior, the susceptance is plotted as <math alttext="B/\omega" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1a"><mrow id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">B</mi><mo id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.1" xref="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml">/</mo><mi id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml">ω</mi></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1b"><apply id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1"><divide id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.1"></divide><ci id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.2">𝐵</ci><ci id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1.1.3">𝜔</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1c">B/\omega</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS1.p3.1.m1.1d">italic_B / italic_ω</annotation></semantics></math>, revealing its asymptotic trend toward the geometric capacitance <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p3.1.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib20" title="">20</a></sup></cite>. Notably, the susceptance in the dark is negative at low frequencies, or pseudo inductive, consistent with the loop in the Nyquist plot <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p3.1.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib27" title="">27</a></sup></cite>.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS1.p4">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS1.p4.3">These dynamics may be associated with recombination pathways and/or electric field compensation effects <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p4.3.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib28" title="">28</a></sup></cite>. To explore these mechanisms further, we measured the current-voltage characteristics under different illumination intensities (Fig. S1a) and conducted capacitance-voltage analysis (Fig. S1c). Evaluating the open-circuit voltage, <math alttext="V_{\text{oc}}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1a"><msub id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">V</mi><mtext id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml">oc</mtext></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1b"><apply id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.2">𝑉</ci><ci id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.3a.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.3"><mtext id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" mathsize="70%" xref="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1.1.3">oc</mtext></ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1c">V_{\text{oc}}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS1.p4.1.m1.1d">italic_V start_POSTSUBSCRIPT oc end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>, as a function of illumination intensity provides valuable insights into recombination kinetics in PSCs. An ideality factor of <math alttext="n=1" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1a"><mrow id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml">n</mi><mo id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.1" xref="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml">=</mo><mn id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml">1</mn></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1b"><apply id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1"><eq id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.1"></eq><ci id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.2">𝑛</ci><cn id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1.1.3">1</cn></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1c">n=1</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS1.p4.2.m2.1d">italic_n = 1</annotation></semantics></math> suggests that second order (bimolecular) charge-carrier recombination dominates, whereas <math alttext="n=2" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1a"><mrow id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1" xref="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml">n</mi><mo id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.1" xref="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.1.cmml">=</mo><mn id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.3.cmml">2</mn></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1b"><apply id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1"><eq id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.1"></eq><ci id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.2">𝑛</ci><cn id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1.1.3">2</cn></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1c">n=2</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS1.p4.3.m3.1d">italic_n = 2</annotation></semantics></math> corresponds to first order (monomolecular) trap-assisted non-radiative recombination. Our devices have an ideality factor of 1.65, aligning with our previous findings using a hysteresis-free device with an inverted architecture <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p4.3.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib29" title="">29</a></sup></cite>. These results for mesoporous PSC suggest that trap-assisted monomolecular recombination dominates near the interfaces, since hysteresis can arise from the combined effect of mobile ions and recombination near the perovskite-contact interfaces <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p4.3.3.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib30" title="">30</a></sup></cite>.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS1.p5">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS1.p5.4">We performed a Mott-Schottky analysis to determine the built-in potential (<math alttext="V_{bi}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1a"><msub id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">V</mi><mrow id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.2" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.2.cmml">b</mi><mo id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.1" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.3" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.3.cmml">i</mi></mrow></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1b"><apply id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.2">𝑉</ci><apply id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3"><times id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.1"></times><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.2">𝑏</ci><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.3">𝑖</ci></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1c">V_{bi}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS1.p5.1.m1.1d">italic_V start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_b italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>) from the capacitance-voltage data (Fig. S1d). The device under investigation exhibited a <math alttext="V_{bi}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1a"><msub id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml">V</mi><mrow id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.2" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.2.cmml">b</mi><mo id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.1" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.3" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.3.cmml">i</mi></mrow></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1b"><apply id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.2">𝑉</ci><apply id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3"><times id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.1"></times><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.2">𝑏</ci><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.3">𝑖</ci></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1c">V_{bi}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS1.p5.2.m2.1d">italic_V start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_b italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> of 0.85 V, lower than that of a previously reported device with a different architecture <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p5.4.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib29" title="">29</a></sup></cite>. A reduced built-in potential typically results in a lower open-circuit voltage, particularly when the contacts are not perfectly selective. This reduced <math alttext="V_{bi}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1a"><msub id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml">V</mi><mrow id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.2" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.2.cmml">b</mi><mo id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.1" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.3" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.3.cmml">i</mi></mrow></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1b"><apply id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.2">𝑉</ci><apply id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3"><times id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.1"></times><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.2">𝑏</ci><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.3">𝑖</ci></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1c">V_{bi}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS1.p5.3.m3.1d">italic_V start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_b italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> may explain why devices that exhibit hysteresis often display lower Voc. The <math alttext="V_{bi}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1"><semantics id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1a"><msub id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.2.cmml">V</mi><mrow id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.2" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.2.cmml">b</mi><mo id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.1" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mi id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.3" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.3.cmml">i</mi></mrow></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1b"><apply id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.2">𝑉</ci><apply id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3"><times id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.1"></times><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.2">𝑏</ci><ci id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1.1.3.3">𝑖</ci></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1c">V_{bi}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS1.p5.4.m4.1d">italic_V start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_b italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> and external bias drive charge carriers toward the electrodes. However, an imbalance in the electron-hole population can cause interfacial charge accumulation, screening the electric field, and reducing charge extraction efficiency <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p5.4.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib31" title="">31</a></sup></cite>. These findings highlight the critical role of built-in potential for ion migration and its impact on device performance <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p5.4.3.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib32" title="">32</a></sup></cite>. Additional details about the ideality factor and the Mott-Schottky analysis are provided in the Supplementary Information (SI).</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS1.p6">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS1.p6.1">While the slow ionic movement within the perovskite layer is widely recognized as a primary cause of the dynamic responses observed in PSCs <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p6.1.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib33" title="">33</a></sup></cite>, IS and equivalent circuit models alone are insufficient to identify the ionic species involved. Our results indicated that bulk ion migration cannot fully account for hysteresis; instead, interfacial processes, which involve the capture and release of localized charges, play a significant role <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p6.1.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib34" title="">34</a></sup></cite>. After diffusion, accumulated ions modify interfacial barriers and impact charge extraction at contacts <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS1.p6.1.3.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib35" title="">35</a></sup></cite>.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S2.SS2">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.2 </span>Theoretical insights</h3>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS2.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p1.1">The conventional approach to analyzing IS data involves fitting the Nyquist plot with an equivalent circuit model and interpreting the physical significance of each circuit element. Yet, it has been reported that equivalent circuit representations may not adequately capture the dynamic behavior of nonlinear systems exhibiting inherent memory effects <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS2.p1.1.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib36" title="">36</a></sup></cite>.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS2.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p2.2">In our previous work <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS2.p2.2.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib20" title="">20</a></sup></cite>, we proposed that a multimode framework might be better suited for a comprehensive spectral analysis as it captures the system’s complexity more effectively. However, in this study we focus on the fundamental mode (<math alttext="m=1" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1a"><mrow id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">m</mi><mo id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml">=</mo><mn id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml">1</mn></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1b"><apply id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1"><eq id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.1"></eq><ci id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.2">𝑚</ci><cn id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1.1.3">1</cn></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1c">m=1</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS2.p2.1.m1.1d">italic_m = 1</annotation></semantics></math>), commonly used in traditional IS analysis, particularly for the <math alttext="j_{D}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1a"><msub id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml">j</mi><mi id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml">D</mi></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1b"><apply id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1.2">𝑗</ci><ci id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1.1.3">𝐷</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1c">j_{D}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS2.p2.2.m2.1d">italic_j start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_D end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math> term in Eq. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S4.E1" title="In 4.2 Analytical model ‣ 4 Experimental ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>. This approach allows for a straightforward comparison between the models.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS2.p3">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p3.1">To accurately represent the complexity of the system, we accounted for multiple concurrent drift channels, each with distinct characteristics, such as varying relaxation times and non-equilibrium carrier transfer dynamics. Under dark conditions, the experimental data was modelled using three distinct memristive channels.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS2.p4">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p4.1">The results from the simulated IS, incorporating contributions from these memristive components, are illustrated in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F2" title="Figure 2 ‣ 2.2 Theoretical insights ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a>. Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F2" title="Figure 2 ‣ 2.2 Theoretical insights ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a>a and Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F2" title="Figure 2 ‣ 2.2 Theoretical insights ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a>b display the Bode plots for conductance and susceptance, respectively, under dark conditions at constant voltage. These plots reveal the individual and combined responses of each memristive channel. Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F2" title="Figure 2 ‣ 2.2 Theoretical insights ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a>c represents the corresponding Nyquist plot for the combined channel conditions under a fixed DC bias in the dark.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS2.p5">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p5.3">At low frequencies, the experimental trends - namely, the rise in conductance and the negative susceptance observed in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F1" title="Figure 1 ‣ 2.1 Experimental results ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>f and Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F1" title="Figure 1 ‣ 2.1 Experimental results ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>g - were successfully reproduced by tuning the parameters in Eq. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S4.E5" title="In 4.2 Analytical model ‣ 4 Experimental ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span></a>, specifically setting <math alttext="\eta&lt;0" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1a"><mrow id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">η</mi><mo id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml">&lt;</mo><mn id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml">0</mn></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1b"><apply id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1"><lt id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.1"></lt><ci id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.2">𝜂</ci><cn id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1.1.3">0</cn></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1c">\eta&lt;0</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS2.p5.1.m1.1d">italic_η &lt; 0</annotation></semantics></math> and <math alttext="\alpha=0.9" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1a"><mrow id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml">α</mi><mo id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml">=</mo><mn id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml">0.9</mn></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1b"><apply id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1"><eq id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.1"></eq><ci id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.2">𝛼</ci><cn id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml" type="float" xref="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1.1.3">0.9</cn></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1c">\alpha=0.9</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS2.p5.2.m2.1d">italic_α = 0.9</annotation></semantics></math>. As demonstrated in our earlier work <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS2.p5.3.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib20" title="">20</a></sup></cite>, the negative susceptance originates from the reactive contribution of the system. The slight asymmetry introduced by <math alttext="\alpha=0.9" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1a"><mrow id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml">α</mi><mo id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.1" xref="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.1.cmml">=</mo><mn id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.cmml">0.9</mn></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1b"><apply id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1"><eq id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.1"></eq><ci id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.2">𝛼</ci><cn id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.3.cmml" type="float" xref="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1.1.3">0.9</cn></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1c">\alpha=0.9</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS2.p5.3.m3.1d">italic_α = 0.9</annotation></semantics></math> causes the sign of the generation function to depend on the polarity of the applied bias. Expanded expressions for these terms are detailed in Ref. <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS2.p5.3.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib20" title="">20</a></sup></cite>.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS2.p6">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p6.1">Our model naturally captures the observed transition from capacitive to inductive response, as illustrated in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F2" title="Figure 2 ‣ 2.2 Theoretical insights ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a>. This eliminates the need to fit IS data using an equivalent circuit that includes an explicit inductive element, a practice commonly found in the literature <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS2.p6.1.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib37" title="">37</a></sup></cite>. By modeling the system with memristive components, we provide a more physically meaningful interpretation of the IS data without invoking inductive elements, which often lack physical justification in the context of solar cells.</p>
</div>
<figure class="ltx_figure" id="S2.F2"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_portrait" height="984" id="S2.F2.g1" src="x2.png" width="653"/>
<figcaption class="ltx_caption ltx_centering"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 2: </span>Simulated spectral in the dark and under illumination at a fixed DC voltage. (a,d) conductance spectrum, (b,e) susceptance, and (c,f) the corresponding Nyquist maps. The inset of (f) presents the circuit from Eq. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S4.E1" title="In 4.2 Analytical model ‣ 4 Experimental ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>.</figcaption>
</figure>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS2.p7">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p7.1">This model effectively captures the influence of illumination on the impedance response of the solar cell, particularly its impact on the reactive components <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS2.p7.1.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib20" title="">20</a></sup></cite>. Illumination modifies the dynamic response by altering the effective barrier heights of the memristive channels. This effect is illustrated in Figs. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F2" title="Figure 2 ‣ 2.2 Theoretical insights ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a>d-f, where the Bode and Nyquist plots depict the impedance response with the addition of a fourth channel to simulate the experimental data under illumination, as represented by the circuit diagram shown in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F2" title="Figure 2 ‣ 2.2 Theoretical insights ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a>f and governed by Eq. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S4.E1" title="In 4.2 Analytical model ‣ 4 Experimental ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS2.p8">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p8.1">Under illumination, the additional channel dominates the low-frequency region and is associated with photogenerated charges carriers. This results in notable effects: a decrease in conductance, a shift to positive susceptance, and the appearance of a second semicircle in the Nyquist plot. These features reflect altered charge dynamics introduced by the photogenerated carriers.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS2.p9">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS2.p9.1">While the present model is not strictly quantitative, it successfully predicts the qualitative trends in the electrical response of perovskite solar cells. Furthermore, this framework offers flexibility for analyzing the system response under other experimental conditions, such as the electrical response to pulsed inputs or purely AC voltage excitations. By extending beyond traditional IS methods, this approach provides a versatile tool for examining additional PSCs dynamics.</p>
</div>
</section>
<section class="ltx_subsection" id="S2.SS3">
<h3 class="ltx_title ltx_title_subsection">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.3 </span>Transient response and AC stimuli</h3>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.p1.1">Dynamic processes are critical for the stabilization of PSCs, particularly under large excitation <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p1.1.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib6" title="">6</a></sup></cite>. We propose an alternative protocol to IS, by utilizing a two-step methodology: (i) a voltage pulse to identify key time constants, and (ii) a sinusoidal voltage input instead of the traditional triangular voltage ramp. The sinusoidal stimulus is used for detailed frequency-dependent dynamics, analogous to but complementary to conventional IS. Despite the illumination effects discussed in previous sections, we focus here on dark conditions, where the inductive-like characteristic is observed. Additionally, in the absence of light, charge carriers are injected solely from the contacts, making the system less susceptible to photodegradation effects <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p1.1.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib26" title="">26</a></sup></cite>.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS3.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.p2.1">Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F3" title="Figure 3 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a> illustrates the current response to 1 second voltage pulses under different amplitudes in the dark. Under a 0.7 V pulse (Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F3" title="Figure 3 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>a), the device exhibits an initial microsecond-scale spike followed by a steady-state current. When the bias exceeds the built-in potential (<math alttext="V_{bi}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1a"><msub id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml">V</mi><mrow id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.2" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.2.cmml">b</mi><mo id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.1" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mi id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.3" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.3.cmml">i</mi></mrow></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1b"><apply id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.2">𝑉</ci><apply id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3"><times id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.1"></times><ci id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.2">𝑏</ci><ci id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1.1.3.3">𝑖</ci></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1c">V_{bi}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p2.1.m1.1d">italic_V start_POSTSUBSCRIPT italic_b italic_i end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>), the current spike is followed by a gradual increase (Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F3" title="Figure 3 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>b). This so-called “negative transient spike” is typically both voltage- and light -dependent (Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F3" title="Figure 3 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>c) <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p2.1.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib11" title="">11</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib38" title="">38</a></sup></cite>.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS3.p3">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.p3.2">Figures <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F3" title="Figure 3 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>d-f decompose the response to a 1.1 V pulse into three distinct timescales, corresponding to capacitive and “inductive” responses as commonly described in the literature <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p3.2.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib39" title="">39</a></sup></cite>. The transient response comprises an initial fast spike, a subsequent current decay, and a slower current increase that stabilizes over time. The fastest process ( 1 <math alttext="\mu" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1.1.cmml">μ</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1.1">𝜇</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1c">\mu</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p3.1.m1.1d">italic_μ</annotation></semantics></math>s), shown in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F3" title="Figure 3 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>d, reflects a pure capacitive effect. Fitting this response with the voltage derivative with respect to the current yields a capacitance of <math alttext="\approx" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1a"><mo id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1.1.cmml">≈</mo><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1b"><approx id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1.1"></approx></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1c">\approx</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p3.2.m2.1d">≈</annotation></semantics></math>18.5 nF, consistent with the value extracted via IS in the frequency range of 10000 and 100000 Hz. Figures <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F3" title="Figure 3 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>e and <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F3" title="Figure 3 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>f illustrate the subsequent current decay and the onset of slower current growth, respectively.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS3.p4">

(-\frac{t}{\tau_{3}})</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p4.1.m1.7d">italic_f ( italic_x ) = italic_A + italic_b start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_exp ( - divide start_ARG italic_t end_ARG start_ARG italic_τ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 1 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_ARG ) + italic_b start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_exp ( - divide start_ARG italic_t end_ARG start_ARG italic_τ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 2 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_ARG ) + italic_b start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 3 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT roman_exp ( - divide start_ARG italic_t end_ARG start_ARG italic_τ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 3 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT end_ARG )</annotation></semantics></math>. Despite being usually related to the capacitive and inductive elements, we assume that the decay and growth exponents correspond to a competition of trapping rates and carrier generation, as described by Eq. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S4.E5" title="In 4.2 Analytical model ‣ 4 Experimental ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span></a> <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p4.3.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib20" title="">20</a></sup></cite>. A capacitance plot and a table summarizing the exponential parameters are provided in the SI. We highlight the third term, where the negative value of b<sub class="ltx_sub" id="S2.SS3.p4.3.2">3</sub> indicates that carrier generation processes become predominant above the characteristic time (<math alttext="\tau_{3}" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1a"><msub id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1.cmml"><mi id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml">τ</mi><mn id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1.3.cmml">3</mn></msub><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1b"><apply id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1">subscript</csymbol><ci id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1.2">𝜏</ci><cn id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1.3.cmml" type="integer" xref="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1.1.3">3</cn></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1c">\tau_{3}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p4.3.m3.1d">italic_τ start_POSTSUBSCRIPT 3 end_POSTSUBSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>) of about 20 ms.</p>

<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS3.p5">

12 end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_s</annotation></semantics></math> to <math alttext="10^{-6}\,s" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1a"><mrow id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.cmml"><msup id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml"><mn id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.2" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.2.cmml">10</mn><mrow id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3.cmml"><mo id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3a" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3.cmml">−</mo><mn id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3.2" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3.2.cmml">6</mn></mrow></msup><mo id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.1" lspace="0.170em" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml">⁢</mo><mi id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.3" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml">s</mi></mrow><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1b"><apply id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1"><times id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.1"></times><apply id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2"><csymbol cd="ambiguous" id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2">superscript</csymbol><cn id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.2.cmml" type="integer" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.2">10</cn><apply id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3"><minus id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3"></minus><cn id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3.2.cmml" type="integer" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.2.3.2">6</cn></apply></apply><ci id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.3.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1.1.3">𝑠</ci></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1c">10^{-6}\,s</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p5.2.m2.1d">10 start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT - 6 end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT italic_s</annotation></semantics></math>, are attributed to electronic processes, while slower dynamics, lasting over 1 second, are associated with electrochemical or ionic processes <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p5.2.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib37" title="">37</a></sup></cite>. The microsecond-scale capacitive response observed in our experiments (Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F3" title="Figure 3 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>d) may be linked to recombination processes either at the interfaces or within the perovskite bulk. As discussed in Section 3.1, an ideality factor of 1.65 suggests that while trap-assisted recombination contributes to the overall response, it is not the dominant recombination mechanism.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS3.p6">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.p6.1">We infer that the slower processes, exceeding 1 s, are associated with ionic motion. This assignment is supported by comparing the timescale of the current decay in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F3" title="Figure 3 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>e, 0.1 to 2 ms, with the negative susceptance below 1000 Hz in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F1" title="Figure 1 ‣ 2.1 Experimental results ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>g. The origin of these slow dynamics may involve relatively fast Li<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p6.1.1">+</sup> ions from the spiro transport layer, slower movement of heavier ions between the perovskite and transport layers, or the formation of ion vacancies at grain boundaries in the perovskite film <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p6.1.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib40" title="">40</a></sup></cite>. It is important to note that the presence of transients imposes additional challenges for spectral analysis as highlighted by Lopez-Richard et al. <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p6.1.3.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib36" title="">36</a></sup></cite>.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS3.p7">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.p7.1">From the dark transient response, our focus is on the current response at voltage biases around 1V and processes occurring on milliseconds. Figure <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F4" title="Figure 4 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a> displays the steady-state current-voltage characteristics in the dark, measured using a sinusoidal voltage input of 1 V at two distinct frequencies: 0.01 Hz and 1 Hz, Figs. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F4" title="Figure 4 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a>a-b. The Sinus-J-V measurement analysis is detailed in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F4" title="Figure 4 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a>c. In this figure, a frequency dependent hysteresis effect is visible. Depending on the voltammetry and IS data, hysteresis could usually be classified as capacitive or inductive. For instance, the counterclockwise hysteresis and the low-frequency loop at Nyquist plot are both indicative of an inductive-like response <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p7.1.1.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib15" title="">15</a></sup></cite>. In previous work <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p7.1.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib20" title="">20</a></sup></cite>, we observed that the frequency tunning of clockwise and counter-clockwise current-voltage loops results from combinations of nonequilibrium carrier trapping and generation, respectively. These behaviors can be described as capacitive or inductive based on the apparent anticipation or delay of the current with respect to the voltage sweep. Therefore, the low frequency curve in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F4" title="Figure 4 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a> has an inductive-like response attributed to nonequilibrium charges, coexisting with a capacitive transport component that is delimited by a transition point <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p7.1.3.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib26" title="">26</a></sup></cite>. At higher frequencies, the influence of nonequilibrium charges weakens and the capacitive response dominates. As marked by the pink circles in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F4" title="Figure 4 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a>c, the transition points also depend on the frequency, being at 0.61 V and 0.87 V for the low and high frequency curves, respectively.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS3.p8">

id="S2.SS3.p8.5.m5.1.1.4.3.1.cmml" xref="S2.SS3.p8.5.m5.1.1.4.3"></minus><cn id="S2.SS3.p8.5.m5.1.1.4.3.2.cmml" type="integer" xref="S2.SS3.p8.5.m5.1.1.4.3.2">1</cn></apply></apply></apply></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.SS3.p8.5.m5.1c">4\,Vs^{-1}</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.SS3.p8.5.m5.1d">4 italic_V italic_s start_POSTSUPERSCRIPT - 1 end_POSTSUPERSCRIPT</annotation></semantics></math>, respectively. This trend aligns with the observations by Thiesbrummel et al. <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p8.6.2.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib42" title="">42</a></sup></cite>, who reported a similar frequency-dependent hysteresis effect between 0.1 and 100 V s<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p8.6.3"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p8.6.3.1">-1</span></sup>. Thus, hysteresis is minimal at slower scan rates (low frequency) and becomes more pronounced at higher scan rates. At further higher scan rates, it would be expected that the ion-freeze effect mitigates hysteresis because ions cannot respond to rapid perturbations.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS3.p9">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.p9.5">The map of polar coordinates in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F4" title="Figure 4 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a>d shows how the phase shift between voltage and current aligns with the capacitive and inductive hysteresis response observed in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F4" title="Figure 4 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a>c. The plot was obtained by using the Fast Fourier Transform (FFT) to convert the time-resolved sinusoidal data to the frequency domain, in which the current amplitude is related to the first harmonic of Fig. S2. The dashed line marks the input voltage phase, arbitrarily positioned at 0<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p9.5.1"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p9.5.1.1">o</span></sup>. The excitation frequency significantly influences the current phase. At 1 Hz, the current phase is 20<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p9.5.2"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p9.5.2.1">o</span></sup>, while at 0.01 Hz the phase is shifted to -2.4<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p9.5.3"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p9.5.3.1">o</span></sup>. Fig. S4e presents the map that highlights the range of 330<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p9.5.4"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p9.5.4.1">o</span></sup> to 30<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p9.5.5"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p9.5.5.1">o</span></sup>. This phase analysis supports the conclusion that the dark transients exhibit inductive-like responses at slower timescales and capacitive responses at faster timescales, as demonstrated in Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F3" title="Figure 3 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>.</p>
</div>
<figure class="ltx_figure" id="S2.F4"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" height="567" id="S2.F4.g1" src="x4.png" width="817"/>
<figcaption class="ltx_caption ltx_centering"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 4: </span>Experimental current and voltage time response in the dark under a sinusoidal voltage of 1V applied to the PSC with (a) 0.01 Hz and (b) 1 Hz. (c) Current-voltage characteristics for the two frequencies. The arrows indicate the scan direction, while the pink circles highlight the transition from a capacitive (clockwise) to an inductive-like response (counter-clockwise). (d) The polar map extracted from the FFT represents the capacitive and inductive-like response on the current phase compared to the input voltage.</figcaption>
</figure>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS3.p10">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.p10.2">Our model, considering only the fundamental harmonic and a single drift channel, is represented by the circuit diagram shown in the inset of Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F5" title="Figure 5 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span></a>c. The simulated curves assume a scenario where a stationary DC voltage is superimposed on an AC voltage component. Figures 5a-b show the current response for two AC voltage conditions. The Sinus-J-V analysis and the polar coordinate map in Figs. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F5" title="Figure 5 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span></a>c and <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F5" title="Figure 5 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span></a>d closely match the experimental data, validating the direct comparison between model and experiments. At higher frequency, the current exhibits a phase of 16.8<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p10.2.1"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p10.2.1.1">o</span></sup>, while at lower frequency the phase shifted to -1.7<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p10.2.2"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S2.SS3.p10.2.2.1">o</span></sup>. The requirement for only one drift channel suggests that the lag in output current under faster perturbations corresponds to a dominant process with a characteristic response time. The simulated curves also show the transition point for the slower sweep, highlighting the inductive-like character at such condition, alternatively visualized by the polar map of Fig. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S2.F5" title="Figure 5 ‣ 2.3 Transient response and AC stimuli ‣ 2 Results and discussion ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span></a>d. Therefore, our model successfully simulates the transition from capacitive to inductive-like without using equivalent circuits with inductive elements. Instead, the simple analytical approach is based purely on charge trapping and generation. Additional details, including Fourier transform outputs for experimental and simulated data, are provided in the SI.</p>
</div>
<figure class="ltx_figure" id="S2.F5"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" height="544" id="S2.F5.g1" src="x5.png" width="817"/>
<figcaption class="ltx_caption ltx_centering"><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 5: </span>Simulated current and voltage time response in the dark under an applied sinusoidal voltage of 1 V with (a) 0.1/<math alttext="\tau" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.F5.3.m1.1"><semantics id="S2.F5.3.m1.1b"><mi id="S2.F5.3.m1.1.1" xref="S2.F5.3.m1.1.1.cmml">τ</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.F5.3.m1.1c"><ci id="S2.F5.3.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.F5.3.m1.1.1">𝜏</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.F5.3.m1.1d">\tau</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.F5.3.m1.1e">italic_τ</annotation></semantics></math> and (b) 10/<math alttext="\tau" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S2.F5.4.m2.1"><semantics id="S2.F5.4.m2.1b"><mi id="S2.F5.4.m2.1.1" xref="S2.F5.4.m2.1.1.cmml">τ</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S2.F5.4.m2.1c"><ci id="S2.F5.4.m2.1.1.cmml" xref="S2.F5.4.m2.1.1">𝜏</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S2.F5.4.m2.1d">\tau</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S2.F5.4.m2.1e">italic_τ</annotation></semantics></math>. (c) Current-voltage characteristic for the two frequencies. The arrows indicate the scan direction, while the pink circle highlights the transition from a capacitive (clockwise) to an inductive-like response (counter-clockwise). (d) The polar map extracted from the FFT represents the capacitive and inductive-like response on the current phase compared to the input voltage. Inset in (c) is the circuit diagram of Eq. <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#S4.E1" title="In 4.2 Analytical model ‣ 4 Experimental ‣ A New Approach to Characterize Charge Transport and Hysteresis in Perovskite Solar Cells"><span class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>. </figcaption>
</figure>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S2.SS3.p11">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S2.SS3.p11.3">Further work would be focused on the analysis of sinus-J-V and FFT under illumination, which will enable better comparison to realistic solar cell measurement protocols. The advantage of using sinus-J-V is that capacitive hysteresis manifests itself as an oval-shaped current-voltage curve. The large widening corresponds to the characteristic response time of the dominant charge transport mechanism or species related to trapping phenomena. On the other hand, the inductive-like response manifest by changing the loop direction above a specific transition point. In our case, the characteristic response time for the charge generation is from tens of milliseconds to seconds, supporting ion migration as the primary non-electronic cause of hysteresis. This is consistent with the mesoporous architecture and perovskite composition, since the iodide ions are relatively small compared to the lattice structure and require less activation energy for migration compared to other intrinsic ions such as Cs<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p11.3.1">+</sup>, FA<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p11.3.2">+</sup> or Br<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p11.3.3">-</sup> <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p11.3.4.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib43" title="">43</a></sup></cite>. Although the exact migrating species remains debated <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p11.3.5.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib44" title="">44</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib45" title="">45</a>, <a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib46" title="">46</a></sup></cite>, current understanding suggests that these ions reduce charge collection efficiency by screening the internal electric field <cite class="ltx_cite ltx_citemacro_cite"><sup class="ltx_sup" id="S2.SS3.p11.3.6.1"><a class="ltx_ref" href="https://arxiv.org/html/2502.10805v1#bib.bib42" title="">42</a></sup></cite>. Therefore, sinus-J-V combined with the proposed analytical model is particularly helpful as it captures the frequency-dependent dynamics of ionic migration more accurately than triangular voltage sweeps and offers deeper insights into the nonlinear process beyond the limitations of conventional small signal impedance spectroscopy method.</p>
</div>
</section>
</section>
<section class="ltx_section" id="S3">
<h2 class="ltx_title ltx_title_section">
<span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">3 </span>Conclusion</h2>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S3.p1">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.p1.1">In conclusion, we present a unified theoretical framework for analyzing charge transport in PSCs, grounded in experimental data from a standard n-i-p mesoporous device. Using three independent memristive transport channels, we effectively numerically derived the IS in the dark. Under illumination, a fourth memristive channel was critical to accurately simulate the IS response.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S3.p2">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.p2.1">In addition to IS, our model can be used to simulate the electrical response of PSC under both pulsed and sinusoidal large stimuli. The transient response to voltage pulses in the dark revealed at least three distinct processes: a rapid capacitive spike, an initial current decay, and a subsequent long-term increase. The first effect occurs at 10 <math alttext="\mu" class="ltx_Math" display="inline" id="S3.p2.1.m1.1"><semantics id="S3.p2.1.m1.1a"><mi id="S3.p2.1.m1.1.1" xref="S3.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml">μ</mi><annotation-xml encoding="MathML-Content" id="S3.p2.1.m1.1b"><ci id="S3.p2.1.m1.1.1.cmml" xref="S3.p2.1.m1.1.1">𝜇</ci></annotation-xml><annotation encoding="application/x-tex" id="S3.p2.1.m1.1c">\mu</annotation><annotation encoding="application/x-llamapun" id="S3.p2.1.m1.1d">italic_μ</annotation></semantics></math>s, corresponding to a capacitance of around 18.5 nF. The second process dominates around 2 ms, while the third becomes significant when it exceeds 20 ms.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S3.p3">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.p3.3">The dark Sinus-J-V through FFT analysis presents the system transition from capacitive to pseudo inductive dynamics, based on amplitude and phase, yielding the trapping and generation of charges as the predominant mechanism. At a low frequency of 0.01 Hz, the input voltage and the current response presented a phase of -2.41<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S3.p3.3.1"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S3.p3.3.1.1">o</span></sup>, resulting in minimal capacitive hysteresis. In contrast, at 1.0 Hz, a phase lag of 20<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S3.p3.3.2"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S3.p3.3.2.1">o</span></sup> between the input voltage and the current response, corresponding to an increase in hysteresis. The simulated results closely mirrored this behavior, predicting a phase lag of 17<sup class="ltx_sup" id="S3.p3.3.3"><span class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic" id="S3.p3.3.3.1">o</span></sup> at the higher frequency.</p>
</div>
<div class="ltx_para" id="S3.p4">
<p class="ltx_p" id="S3.p4.1">Notably, our model accurately predicted the electrical response of real devices without relying on conventional equivalent circuit models. This approach offers a novel and comprehensive understanding of hysteresis phenomena in PSCs and paves the way for more robust and versatile methods to characterize these systems.</p>
</div>
</section>
